    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1833163199" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4365850524875173226">The last couple of years, I've made an effort to fit in a few classic plays between novels and am still amazed at how much I enjoy them. Of the 15 or so I've read, I do believe <b>ARSENIC AND OLD LACE</b> has taken over first place on my favorites list.<p><b>The old Brewster ladies are really a hoot!</b> Home is Brooklyn and Martha and Abby are set in their ways with their cadaverous routines, but just as sweet as can be <i>(hehehe)</i> as they welcome prospective lodgers in for a glass of their own special blend of el</p></span>
  <span id="freeText4365850524875173226" style="display:none">The last couple of years, I've made an effort to fit in a few classic plays between novels and am still amazed at how much I enjoy them. Of the 15 or so I've read, I do believe <b>ARSENIC AND OLD LACE</b> has taken over first place on my favorites list.<p><b>The old Brewster ladies are really a hoot!</b> Home is Brooklyn and Martha and Abby are set in their ways with their cadaverous routines, but just as sweet as can be <i>(hehehe)</i> as they welcome prospective lodgers in for a glass of their own special blend of elderberry wine.</p><p>Nephew Teddy <i>(Roosevelt that is)</i> resides in the Brewster home too <b>CHARGING</b> around reeking havoc blowing his bugle as he plans attacks on the enemy often retreating down <i>(the cellar)</i> to Panama to dig locks for the canal.</p><p>
  <b>Did I mention that insanity runs rampant in the Brewster family?</b>
</p><p>Nephew Mortimer often shows up to check on the "old gals" and Teddy, but it's not until he begins searching for the misplaced envelope that trouble really starts to brew!</p><p>Wonderfully funny! Superb Ending! <i>(hehehe)</i></p><p>If you have a couple spare hours or need a break from a chunkster you're reading or just need a few laughs, I highly recommend <b>ARSENIC AND OLD LACE!</b></p></span>

<div id="review_20738845" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/20738845" />
    <a title="Kat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1008175-kat"><img alt="Kat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1370231937p2/1008175.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/20738845?book_show_action=true">Apr 22, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kat" href="/user/show/1008175-kat">Kat</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1008175-kat?shelf=plays">plays</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1008175-kat?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1008175-kat?shelf=books-i-ve-taught">books-i-ve-taught</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1008175-kat?shelf=2011">2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1008175-kat?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1008175-kat?shelf=2004">2004</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer20738845" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18073421610101730769">Basic plot: two little old ladies while away their golden years by taking care of their nephew Teddy and actively pursuing their charity work. Never mind that Teddy thinks he's Teddy Roosevelt and their charity work consists of poisoning little old men.<br/><br/>This is one of the most wonderful bits of dark humor I've ever read. It's laugh-out-loud funny (even when just reading it) and still manages to be creepy. Oh the irony, oh the death, oh the references to Boris Karloff...<br/><br/>I just got done reading thi</span>
  <span id="freeText18073421610101730769" style="display:none">Basic plot: two little old ladies while away their golden years by taking care of their nephew Teddy and actively pursuing their charity work. Never mind that Teddy thinks he's Teddy Roosevelt and their charity work consists of poisoning little old men.<br /><br />This is one of the most wonderful bits of dark humor I've ever read. It's laugh-out-loud funny (even when just reading it) and still manages to be creepy. Oh the irony, oh the death, oh the references to Boris Karloff...<br /><br />I just got done reading this play again, after reading it with my technical theatre class in preparation for their final project. I am once again struck by how well the tale was crafted. The story is still funny to me, even after multiple readings. The inside jokes within the play for theatre people (Mortimer has some great lines, as he's supposed to be a theatre critic) are hysterical. In this 21st century world, we don't tend to think of how sassy people used to be in the 1930's and 1940's. We forget that the people of that time were human beings, with needs/wants and senses of humor, justice, or the macabre. This play reminds us that our grandparents were young once, too, and were people we actually CAN relate to.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18073421610101730769" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780822200659" href="/book/show/790375.Arsenic_and_Old_Lace_Acting_Edition">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59357-suvi?shelf=_north-america">_north-america</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59357-suvi?shelf=theatre">theatre</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59357-suvi?shelf=crime-mystery">crime-mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59357-suvi?shelf=1900-1939">1900-1939</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59357-suvi?shelf=_book-shelf">_book-shelf</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2746394209" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12720516926177500040"><blockquote>
  <i>ABBY. His name's Hoskins - Adam Hoskins. That's really all I know about him - except that he's a Methodist.<br/>MORTIMER. That's all you know about him? Well, what's he doing here? What happened to him?<br/>MARTHA. He died.<br/>MORTIMER. Aunt Martha, men don't just get into window seats and die.<br/>ABBY. (Silly boy.) No, he died first.<br/>MORTIMER. Well, how?<br/>ABBY. Oh, Mortimer, don't be so inquisitive. The gentleman died because he drank some wine with poison in it.<br/>MORTIMER. How did the poison get in the wine?<br/>MARTH</i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText12720516926177500040" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>ABBY. His name's Hoskins - Adam Hoskins. That's really all I know about him - except that he's a Methodist.<br />MORTIMER. That's all you know about him? Well, what's he doing here? What happened to him?<br />MARTHA. He died.<br />MORTIMER. Aunt Martha, men don't just get into window seats and die.<br />ABBY. (Silly boy.) No, he died first.<br />MORTIMER. Well, how?<br />ABBY. Oh, Mortimer, don't be so inquisitive. The gentleman died because he drank some wine with poison in it.<br />MORTIMER. How did the poison get in the wine?<br />MARTHA. Well, we put it in wine because it's less noticeable - when it's in tea it has a distinct odor.</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />Sweet old ladies doing "charity work", a secret in the cellar, their nephew who thinks he's <b>Theodore Roosevelt</b>, Mortimer who's ready to have a nervous breakdown because of his aunts, a minister's daughter, other nephew who looks suspiciously like <b>Boris Karloff</b> (this nephew was actually played by Karloff on Broadway), and Dr. Einstein (not THAT Einstein).<br /><br />A hilarious play that is great as a "snack read". Fun fact: I detest <b>Frank Capra</b>'s films, but he did a great job with the adaptation. The casting is top notch as well. <b>Peter Lorre</b> and <b>Cary Grant</b> in the same movie? Yay!</span>

<div id="review_1127021158" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1127021158" />
    <a title="Samantha" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5856236-samantha"><img alt="Samantha" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1313368237p2/5856236.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1127021158?book_show_action=true">Dec 08, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Samantha" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Samantha" href="/user/show/5856236-samantha">Samantha</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780822200659" href="/book/show/790375.Arsenic_and_Old_Lace_Acting_Edition">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1127021158" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2872905058731300718">One of the Common Core State Standards is to look at irony and understatement. This play is a great way to acquaint students with those literary techniques. One of my classes hated the play, but the other class got really into it and began acting it out. When I would tell them we were going to stop, they would ask to read JUST ONE MORE PAGE! It was a good time. The play is cute and funny. Abby and Martha are wonderful characters. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1088111271" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1088111271" />
    <a title="Realini" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7873218-realini"><img alt="Realini" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420269105p2/7873218.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1088111271?book_show_action=true">Oct 24, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Realini" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Realini" href="/user/show/7873218-realini">Realini</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1088111271" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3158153495010706619">Arsenic and Old Lace by Joseph Kesselring<br/>A family of lunatics goes on a rampage killing 24 people between them. And they call it a comedy.<br/>Actually, rated among the best ever…<br/><br/>A horror comedy might be a way to describe this crime story with plenty of laughs.<br/>A family of lunatics goes on a rampage killing 24 people between them.<br/>How can you turn this into a comedy?<br/>It can’t be easy, but Frank Capra, with the help of Cary Grant has turned this into one of the best comedy films ever made, on some lists </span>
  <span id="freeText3158153495010706619" style="display:none">Arsenic and Old Lace by Joseph Kesselring<br />A family of lunatics goes on a rampage killing 24 people between them. And they call it a comedy.<br />Actually, rated among the best ever…<br /><br />A horror comedy might be a way to describe this crime story with plenty of laughs.<br />A family of lunatics goes on a rampage killing 24 people between them.<br />How can you turn this into a comedy?<br />It can’t be easy, but Frank Capra, with the help of Cary Grant has turned this into one of the best comedy films ever made, on some lists among the top 10.<br />I have known the movie and this morning I have heard the play that is based on. <br />It seems a bit farfetched and it made me think of the absurd theater of Eugen Ionesco- we do not have rhinoceros roaming the streets, but there are a couple of old aunts that kill twelve men, a mental case that thinks he is president Teddy Roosevelt and another cuckoo who has killed a dozen people in his turn.<br />	“Hell is paved with good intentions”<br />The couple of old ladies- Martha and Abby Brewster mean well. They meet lonely men and offer them an escape from this wretched life of solitude with…Arsenic.<br />Mortimer Brewster is the nephew, who is shocked to find out about the “Charity work” that his aunts are doing. He is so worried about having “Teddy Roosevelt”, a crazy brother – Jonathan and now a couple of crazy aunts that he wants to cancel his planned wedding to Elaine Harper.<br />There is a romantic side to the comedy and Elaine Harper adds spice to the plot by stumbling upon Jonathan, the lunatic brother who comes home with another dead body to add to the twelve already lying around the house.<br />Mortimer is trying his best to protect Elaine from all the madness and killing that is going around, but he is only making things worse for their relationship by asking his loved one to leave the house.<br />-	Get out! Is all right maybe once, but he has to send his would be wife packing a few times, as the crazies in his family are busy doing what they know best: mischief.<br />Overwhelmed, Mortimer gives up on his marriage plans:<br />-	I have only crazy people in my family, we must not marry<br />Or something along those lines.<br />The plot thickens when Jonathan shows up with doctor…Einstein. The first reaction is to think there’s another lunatic in the Teddy Roosevelt mould. But no, this is one is different- he does not want to pass for the famous Albert.<br />However, crazy he is. He performs operations on Jonathan, who keeps changing appearance after each, or the most serious of his crimes. They even reach the funny (?) state where they compare and race each other on the number of murders.<br />The “Old Lace” is responsible for twelve murders and the nephew has another dozen under his belt. But the police come in, alas, only to add to the confusion and hilarity.<br />The local policeman is a…part time writer:<br />-	My job as a policeman is only temporary<br />-	Really? How long have you been doing it?<br />-	Twelve years<br />The amateur play writer starts telling his stories and never notices the dead people and the con artists hanging all around. But then the main intention is comedy, even if at times I wondered- should I laugh or be horrified. <br />There are moments when I smiled no matter how morbid the talk was…like when the old ladies refused to have a dead stranger in with their own beloved twelve victims.<br />The cellar is where the dead bodies are, which is called The Panama Canal, to cheer up and use “Teddy Roosevelt” to carry and burry the corpses.<br />When the local head of the police finds out about the story of the dead people in the cellar, he dismisses it as another fable, cooked up by these people who claim they are presidents or the like.<br />It is an entertaining read or audition. I heard it in the form of a play, for the BBC- I guess.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3158153495010706619" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2968608472" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2968608472" />
    <a title="Robin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19187494-robin"><img alt="Robin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1365355609p2/19187494.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2968608472?book_show_action=true">Sep 18, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robin" href="/user/show/19187494-robin">Robin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2968608472" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2387760837821660014"><i>Mortimer: You can't do things like that. I don't know how to explain this to you, but it's not only against the law. It's wrong! It's not a nice thing to do...what I mean is--well, this has developed into a very bad habit.<br/><br/>Abby: We don't try to stop you from doing things you like to do.</i><br/><br/>A delightfully dark comedy, <i>Arsenic and Old Lace</i> has everything you could ask for--sweet, murderous old ladies, more funny moving dead body jokes than <i>Weekend at Bernie's</i>, and a pleasantly insane, bugle-playing nep</span>
  <span id="freeText2387760837821660014" style="display:none"><i>Mortimer: You can't do things like that. I don't know how to explain this to you, but it's not only against the law. It's wrong! It's not a nice thing to do...what I mean is--well, this has developed into a very bad habit.<br /><br />Abby: We don't try to stop you from doing things you like to do.</i><br /><br />A delightfully dark comedy, <i>Arsenic and Old Lace</i> has everything you could ask for--sweet, murderous old ladies, more funny moving dead body jokes than <i>Weekend at Bernie's</i>, and a pleasantly insane, bugle-playing nephew who thinks he's Teddy Roosevelt. <br /><br />Unmarried sisters Abby and Martha Brewster are paragons of kindness in their Brooklyn community, famous for their generosity and charity. They seem to be the most morally healthy of the strange Brewster clan--until their dramatic critic nephew Mortimer discovers their penchant for poisoning and burying lonely men in their cellar (this isn't a spoiler, it happens early in Act I). While the overwhelmed Mortimer processes this and tries to keep his amorous fiance at arm's length and his utterly unconcerned aunts out of legal trouble, his psychopathic brother Jonathan returns, not knowing that he may have met his homicidal match in his seemingly innocent aunts. <br /><br />It's a play that revels in dark but not heavy humor, effective repeated jokes, dramatic irony, and subverted expectations. But this isn't high literature here. The "good" characters, even if they happen to be murderers, are all likable, and the villains are all amusingly detestable. <i>Arsenic and Old Lace</i> is meant to be fun, and it's extremely effective in being fun. It also loves riffing on its status as a play, with the the minister, whose daughter is marrying Mortimer, bemoaning his future son in law's connection to the unethical theater, his aunt's insistence that he actually hates it, and Mortimer's own commentary about plays and acting. Through this denigration of its own genre, it delights in breaking the fourth wall, likely before that phrase even existed. <br /><br />The play also would benefit from a re-reading, which I find uncommon in plays and even novels. This is a joke from earlier in the play that we didn't know was a joke, because we didn't know what the aunts were about yet:<br /><br /><i>Harper: Yes, I'm well acquainted with their charities. <br /><br />Klein: You don't know the tenth of it. When I was with the Missing Persons Bureau I was trying to trace an old man that we never did find--do you know there's a renting agency that got this house down on its list for furnished rooms? They don't rent rooms--but you can bet that anybody who comes here lookin' for a room goes away with a good meal and probably a few dollars in their kick. <br /><br />Brophy: It's just their way of digging up people to do some good to.</i><br /><br /><i>Digging.</i> It's easy to like a play that the author clearly had such an enjoyable time writing.<br /><br />I did occasionally find the play frustrating to read with the copious amount of staging directions, and sometimes the rapid-fire exchanges of short dialogue made it confusing to understand who was doing what and going where on the stage. But all these issues would be resolved, I expect, by either watching this as a production or the 1944 movie version. <br /><br />To discuss the play in greater detail would do disservice to the plot, so I think I'll stop here. But I had a lot of fun reading this play, and I imagine most would do likewise. I end this review quoting the immortal words of Teddy Brewster: <b>CHARGE</b>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2387760837821660014" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3564403187" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3564403187" />
    <a title="Abby" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1863790-abby"><img alt="Abby" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1539973693p2/1863790.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3564403187?book_show_action=true">Sep 24, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Abby" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Abby" href="/user/show/1863790-abby">Abby</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3564403187" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer293281225304015165">Arsenic and Old Lace is one of those rare classics that remains funny today. A nephew charging around planning attacks on the enemy in the cellar and two sweet old aunts that keep an eye on him while also taking in lonely lodgers into their Brewster home and doing charity work. Sounds nice enough. But when nephew Mortimer shows up to check on everyone and to search for a missing envelope…trouble starts to brew and he begins to find a darker secret within the house and some interesting effects of</span>
  <span id="freeText293281225304015165" style="display:none">Arsenic and Old Lace is one of those rare classics that remains funny today. A nephew charging around planning attacks on the enemy in the cellar and two sweet old aunts that keep an eye on him while also taking in lonely lodgers into their Brewster home and doing charity work. Sounds nice enough. But when nephew Mortimer shows up to check on everyone and to search for a missing envelope…trouble starts to brew and he begins to find a darker secret within the house and some interesting effects of the Brewster sisters elderberry wine. From an actor perspective, the roles of Abby and Martha are usually dream roles for when actresses get older – they’re full of personality and so freaking funny. Even if you’ve seen the movie or play, reading the script helps you catch even more hidden jokes and hilarious lines. <br /></span>

<div id="review_2022407536" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

<span id="freeTextContainer2136224373897794508">In my class we were assigned to read/act out the play Arsenic and old lace. At first I didn't really like the play at all, but when Jonathan was first introduced I actually wanted to finally read my lines assigned to me in class. Every second I was metaphorically on the edge of my seat when Mortimer was explaining a play to Einstein and Jonathan was slowly coming up behind Mortimer and going for the curtain cord to tie him up. I hope my small review makes you want to try and read this extraordin</span>
  <span id="freeText2136224373897794508" style="display:none">In my class we were assigned to read/act out the play Arsenic and old lace. At first I didn't really like the play at all, but when Jonathan was first introduced I actually wanted to finally read my lines assigned to me in class. Every second I was metaphorically on the edge of my seat when Mortimer was explaining a play to Einstein and Jonathan was slowly coming up behind Mortimer and going for the curtain cord to tie him up. I hope my small review makes you want to try and read this extraordinary piece of writing by Joseph Kesselring.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('jjyu/Ssq3xDCNwGO3/KrZM3783rH6Eg/bY6fe0F3ojjVj747/MID76ppVyOmF2qD7HelsCkp5Px6TRoe/uvGFg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('LFwc+8StgS6qrS8M6Dl+HM28Nriff6AG5nTSl3OhaVl37ww9E0Vd0cLzeaGR3L/77DBgcnG+DMXxt1fyzD0Ndw==')})
  }

The two b<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText819517026011105094\" style=\"display:none\">Noises Off, the classic farce by the Tony Award—winning author of Copenhagen, is not one play but two: simultaneously a traditional sex farce, Nothing On, and the backstage “drama” that develops during Nothing On’s final rehearsal and tour. Then, Aphrodite, taking pity on this man whose love could not reach beyond the barrier of stone, brought the statue to life and gave her to Pygmalion as his bride. Centuries later, George Bernard Shaw captured the magic of this legend in his celebrated play, Pygmalion. Pygmalion became Henry Higgins, a professor of phonetics, his statue an untutored flower girl from the streets of London, and the barrier between them is class.. Shaw expected Eliza to end up with Freddy and run a flower shop.\n\nIn My Fair Lady, Alan Jay Lerner takes the legend one step further—the barrier is swept away and Higgins and Eliza are reunited as Debuting at the Richard Rodgers Theatre in 1990, Lost in Yonkers went on to win four Tony Awards, including Best Play, as well as the Pulitzer Prize, and tells the moving drama about the cruelties and painful memories that scar a family.\n\nIt is New York, 1942. After the death of their mother, two young brothers are sent to stay with their formidable grandmother for the longest ten months of their lives. But it is Bella\'s hunger for affection and her refusal to be denied love that saves the boys—and that leads to an unforgettable, wrenching confrontation with her mother. Having recently demolished another car, Daisy Wertham, a rich, sharp-tongued Jewish widow of seventy-two, is informed by her son, Boolie, that henceforth she must rely on the services of a chauffeur. The person he hires for the job is a thoughtful, unemployed black man, Hoke, whom Miss Daisy immediately regards with disdain and who, in turn, is not impressed with his employer\'s patronizing tone and, he believes, her latent prejudice. But, in a series of absorbing scenes spanning twenty-five years, the two, despite their mutual differences, grow ever closer to, and more dependent on, each other, until, eventually, they become almost a couple. Slowly and steadily the dignified, good-natured Hoke breaks down the stern defenses of the ornery old lady, as she teaches him to read and write and, in a gesture of good will and shared concern, invites him to join her at a banquet in honor of Martin Luther King, Jr. As the play ends Hoke has a final visit with Miss Daisy, now ninety-seven and confined to a nursing home, and while it is evident that a vestige of her fierce independence and sense of position still remain, it is also movingly clear that they have both come to realize they have more in common than they ever believed The play centers on Juror Eight, who is at first the sole holdout in an 11-1 guilty vote. Eight sets his sights not on proving the other jurors wrong but rather on getting them to look at the situation in a clear-eyed way not affected by their personal prejudices or biases. Reginald Rose deliberately and carefully peels away the layers of artifice from the men and allows a fuller picture to form of them—and of America, at its best and worst.   After the critically acclaimed teleplay aired in 1954, this landmark American drama went on to become a cinematic masterpiece in 1957 starring Henry Fonda, for which Rose wrote the adaptation. More recently, Twelve Angry Men had a successful, and award-winning, run on Broadway.For more than seventy years, Penguin has been the leading publisher of classic literature in the English-speaking world. In James Goldman’s classic play The Lion in Winter, domestic turmoil rises to an art form. Keenly self-aware and motivated as much by spite as by any sense of duty, Henry II and Eleanor of Aquitaine maneuver against each other to position their favorite son in line for succession. By imagining the inner lives of Henry, Eleanor, and their sons, John, Geoffrey, and Richard, Goldman created the quintessential drama of family strife and competing ambitions, a work that gives visceral, modern-day relevance to the intrigues of Angevin England. Combining keen historical and psychological insight with delicious, mordant wit, the stage play has become a touchstone of today’s theater scene, and Goldman’s screenplay for the 1968 film adaptation won him an Academy Award.
